rowsep0" align="char" char=".">50.628</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">0.02</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The carbon dioxide adsorption isotherms of CFs and CFs-P15 can be seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c. The specific surface area of CFs-P15 evaluated by the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller equation is 50.628 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and close to that of CFs (47.157 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>), as shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table </a><a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">1</a>, suggesting that the macropores on the surface of CFs-P15 produced by H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> pyrolysis do not obviously affect the specific surface area. Both CFs and CFs-P15 possess low specific surface area, which can avoid forming excess solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films. The pore size distribution of the hard carbon samples was evaluated using the density functional theory calculations. As can be seen from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d, most pores for CFs and CFs-P15 samples distribute in the range of 0.5–0.9 nm, mainly around 0.8 nm.</div><div class="NLM_p">XPS was used to disclose the chemical composition of the CFs-P15 surface. The survey spectrum of CFs-P15 is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a, P 2s and P 2p peaks can be obviously seen besides C 1s and O 1s. The high-resolution C 1s spectrum in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b can be deconvoluted into four peaks and corresponding four forms of carbon species: C–C (284.6 eV, 44.18%), C–O (285.4 eV, 38.3%), O–C═O (287.8 eV, 15.79%), and C–P (283.8 eV, 1.73%). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The high-resolution P 2p spectrum as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c reveals the formation of P–C bond (131.9 eV, 78.73%) and P–O bond (133.8 eV, 21.27%) in CFs-P15. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> These results manifest that the doped phosphorus atoms can incorporate into the carbon framework of CFs-P15 and most of them are connecting with carbon atoms.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05618u_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0004.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Overall X-ray photoelectron spectrum of CFs-P15, (b) X-ray photoelectron spectrum of CFs-P15 in C 1s region, (c) X-ray photoelectron spectrum of CFs-P15 in P 2p region. Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of (d) CFs and (e) CFs-P15 at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, (f) summary of capacity above and below 0.1 V of corresponding samples.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05618&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To investigate the relationship between microstructure and electrochemical performance, the as-prepared electrodes were tested at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and the corresponding discharge/charge profiles are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d,e, respectively. CFs-P15 exhibits an impressive charge specific capacity of 288 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with a higher ICE of 55.7%, compared to 239 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 51.1% of CFs. Note that both CFs and CFs-P15 discharge/charge profiles can be divided into two regions: (a) a flat plateau below 0.1 V, attributing to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into enlarged graphite layers and (b) a slope above 0.1 V corresponding to Na<sup>+</sup> absorption at surface or defects. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> The detailed slope/plateau capacity values are summarized using histogram, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>f. Significantly, the CFs-P15 possesses higher capacities in both plateau region and slope region than CFs. Specifically, P-doped CFs-P15 increases the interlayer spacing between graphite layers, so that the plateau capacity increases from 32 to 54 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Meanwhile, the slope capacity rises from 207 to 234 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which can be attributed to more vacancies or defects in CFs-P15 due to P-doping. In addition, the P–C bond has a higher Na adsorption energy than C, which was proved in our previous work. Hence, the higher capacity of CFs-P15 also can be attributed to the P–C bonds. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a,b presents the CV curves of CFs and CFs-P15 for the first several cycles. Two intense irreversible peaks located at ∼0.6 and 1.2 V can be observed for both electrodes, in the first cathodic scan, which result from the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) film on the surface of electrode. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> In subsequent scans, the two irreversible reduction peaks disappear, which implies that the SEI film mainly forms in the first cycle. At low potential region (0–0.1 V), a pair of sharp redox peaks can be found in every scan, corresponding to the Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/desertion between graphite layers. At the same time, we also note that the pair of redox peaks for CFs-P15 electrode is more sharp and intense, compared with CFs electrode, indicating that the speed of Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/desertion between graphite layers in CFs-P15 electrode is faster than that in CFs, which benefits from the larger interlayer spacing of it. The CV curves show good repeatability after the first scan, implying high reversibility in subsequent cycles.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05618u_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0005.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. CV curves of (a) CFs and (b) CFs-P15 at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05618&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The cycling performance of CFs and CFs-P15 tested at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a, and we can see that the CFs-P15 exhibits a higher reversible capacity of 253 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 200th cycle, corresponding to a higher capacity retention of 87.8%, compared to 192 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 80.3% of CFs. It is worth noting that the CFs-P15 exhibits better cycling stability than CFs, resulting from its more stable structure, the relatively bigger interlayer graphite spacing, and porous morphology, which can not only buffer the stress caused by sodium ions insertion/desertion, but also make sodium ions transport and storage more easy. To prove this conjecture, the cycled morphology of CFs-P15 was investigated by SEM and the corresponding SEM images and EDS mapping images after different cycles at 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>. As shown by the results, the macroporous structure of CFs-P15 is mainly preserved after different cycles; meanwhile, the EDS mapping images reveal the uniform distribution of phosphorus after 200 cycles. Such a good structural stability and uniform distribution of phosphorus should make a great contribution to the superior cycling performance of the CFs-P15.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05618u_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0006.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) Cycle performance of CFs and CFs-P15 at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and (b) rate performance of CFs and CFs-P15.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05618&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig7" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05618u_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0007.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a–c) SEM images of CFs-P15 after 1, 100, and 200 cycles. (d–f) EDS elemental distribution mapping of CFs-P15 (based on (c)).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05618&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate the feasibility of using CFs-P15 as an anode of SIBs in high-power devices, the rate performance of it was further tested by gradually increasing the current density from 50 to 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> and then returning to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup> after cycling at 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). It can be seen that CFs-P15 displays superior rate performance compared with CFs. The CFs-P15 delivers reversible capacities of 287, 269, 253, 228, 149, and 103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 50, 100, 200, 500, 1000, and 2000 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density goes back to 50 mA g<sup>–1</sup>, the capacity of CFs-P15 can return to 289 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, demonstrating excellent reversibility.</div><div class="NLM_p">To understand the reaction kinetics of CFs and CFs-P15, the EIS measurements were carried out and the corresponding Nyquist plots are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a. In high-frequency region, a semicircle can be seen, which represents the charge transfer resistance. To obtain the charge transfer resistances of CFs and CFs-P15, the EIS curves were fitted to an equivalent electric circuit (the inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a) and the corresponding values are 433 and 375.4 Ω, respectively. The lower charge transfer resistance of CFs-P15 is due to the existence of P–C bond, which can increase the electron density around the Fermi level, thus leading to the enhancement of electronic conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54" aria-label="reference 54">(54)</a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>b shows a linear relationship between <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in the low-frequency Warburg region of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">8</a>a. The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na<sup>+</sup></sub>) can be written as <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05618u_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i10"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05618u_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i11"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>R</i> is the ideal gas constant, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> is the surface area of the electrode, <i>n</i> is the number of electrons per molecule during oxidization, <i>F</i> is Faraday’s constant, <i>C</i> is the concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> (4.9 × 10<sup>–4</sup> mol cm<sup>–3</sup>), <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> and σ is the Warburg factor, which is relative to <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>b. The Warburg coefficients are 4970 and 2728 Ω s<sup>–1/2</sup>, respectively. On the other hand, the diffusion coefficient of Na<sup>+</sup> can be calculated on the basis of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eqs 1">eqs </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="1">1</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a>, and the values of CFs and CFs-P15 are 3.18 × 10<sup>–15</sup> and 3.49 × 10<sup>–14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of CFs-P15, caused by the larger interlayer graphite spacing and the existence of macropores in its structure, which can provide shorter diffusion path, can contribute in elevating the rate performance.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/medium/am-2018-05618u_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0008.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy analyses of different hard carbon samples and (b) <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> vs ω<sup>–1/2</sup> plot.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.8b05618/asset/images/large/am-2018-05618u_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.8b05618&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">Hard carbon nanofibers were facilely fabricated by electrospinning, followed by a thermal treatment process. The as-prepared CFs-P15 (doping H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>) can deliver higher reversible capacity of 288 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with higher capacity retention of 87.8% compared with CFs (without H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>). Simultaneously, the CFs-P15 also exhibits superior rate performance (103 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>). By investigating the relationship between microstructure and electrochemical performance, we find that P-doping does increase (002) <i>d</i>-spacing and provides more defects and pores in the structure, which ensures fast and efficient electron transportation. Besides, the large interlayer spacing between graphite layers enormously facilitates sodium-ion transportation and reversible storage, which is crucial to sodium ions due to their sluggish transport kinetics. This work proves that P-doping is a meaningful way to boost the electrochemical performances of hard carbon and the P-doped hard carbon nanofibers is a promising anode material candidate for SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="_suppInfo"></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b05618" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="authorInformationSection"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="authorInformationSection">Author Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><ul><li><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Corresponding Authors</span><ul><li><div class="authorItemInformation"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor strong">Ying Bai</span> - <span class="hlFld-Affiliation affiliation">Beijing Key Laboratory

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of some of the current scientific issues underpinning sodium-ion batteries.  Cathode and anode materials and electrolytes for high-performance sodium-ion batteries as well as computational studies of sodium ion mobility are discussed.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1039/C6CS00776G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hwang, J.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Myung, S.-T</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Y.-K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Sodium-ion Batteries: Present and Future</span>. <i>Chem. Rev.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>46</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3529</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3614</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSRVBR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. A.; Park, Chansun; Lim, Soo Yeon; Kim, Joo-Seong; Jo, Yong Nam; Cho, Woosuk; Miyasaka, Keiichi; Kahraman, Ramazan; Jung, Yousung; Choi, Jang Wook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Considering the promising electrochem. performance of the recently reported pyrophosphate family in Li ion batteries as well as the increasing importance of Na ion batteries (SIBs) for emerging large-scale applications, here, the crystal structure, electrochem. properties, and thermal stability of Na2FeP2O7, the 1st example ever reported in the pyrophosphate family for SIBs, are studied.  Na2FeP2O7 maintains well-defined channel structures (triclinic framework under the P1 space group) and exhibits a reversible capacity of ≈90 mA-h g-1 with good cycling performance.  Both quasi-equil. measurements and 1st-principles calcns. consistently indicate that Na2FeP2O7 undergoes 2 kinds of reactions over the entire voltage range of 2.0-4.5 V (vs. Na/Na+): a single-phase reaction around 2.5 V and 2-phase reactions in the voltage range of 3.0-3.25 V.  Na2FeP2O7 shows excellent thermal stability up to 500°, even in the partially de-sodiated state (NaFeP2O7), which suggests its safe character, a property that is very crit. for large-scale battery applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5358"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oyama, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, S.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A 3.8-V earth-abundant sodium battery electrode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Barpanda, Prabeer; Oyama, Gosuke; Nishimura, Shin-ichi; Chung, Sai-Cheong; Yamada, Atsuo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable lithium batteries have ushered the wireless revolution over last two decades and are now matured to enable green automobiles.  However, the growing concern on scarcity and large-scale applications of lithium resources have steered effort to realize sustainable sodium-ion batteries, Na and Fe being abundant and low-cost charge carrier and redox center, resp.  However, their performance is limited owing to low operating voltage and sluggish kinetics.  Here we report a hitherto-unknown material with entirely new compn. and structure with the first alluaudite-type sulfate framework, Na2Fe2(SO4)3, registering the highest-ever Fe3+/Fe2+ redox potential at 3.8 V (vs. Na, and hence 4.1 V vs. Li) along with fast rate kinetics.  Rare-metal-free Na-ion rechargeable battery system compatible with the present Li-ion battery is now in realistic scope without sacrificing high energy d. and high power, and paves way for discovery of new earth-abundant sustainable cathodes for large-scale batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201600275"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ni, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1600275</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/advs.201600275</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D4%26publication_year%3D2017%26journal%3DAdv.%2BSci.%26author%3DQ.%2BNi%26author%3DY.%2BBai%26author%3DF.%2BWu%26author%3DC.%2BWu%26title%3DPolyanion-Type%2BElectrode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadvs.201600275&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b05618&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadvs.201600275&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Polyanion-Type Electrode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ni Qiao; Bai Ying; Wu Feng; Wu Chuan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced science (Weinheim, Baden-Wurttemberg, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">4</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1600275</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2198-3844</span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.  This paper presents a brief review of the research progress of polyanion-type electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, summarizing recent accomplishments, highlighting emerging strategies, and discussing the remaining challenges of such systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D. These as-prepd. hard carbon samples were used as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  The hard carbon obtained from PVC nanofibers had a high reversible capacity of 271 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 69.9%, which were much superior to the one from com. PVC, namely, a reversible capacity of 206 mA-h/g and an initial coulombic efficiency of 60.9%.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Kelp-derived hard carbons as advanced anode materials for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Pengzi; Zhu, Xiaoshu; Wang, Qiaoqiao; Xu, Xin; Zhou, Xiaosi; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have received much attention for scalable elec. energy storage because of the abundance and wide availability of sodium resources.  However, it is still unclear whether carbon anodes can realize large-scale com. application in SIBs as in lithium-ion batteries.  Recently, great attention has been devoted to hard carbon which has been treated as a promising choice.  Herein, we observe that the turbostratic lattice of kelp-derived hard carbon (KHC) is repeatedly expandable and shrinkable upon cycling, where the interlayer distance varies between 3.9 and 4.3 Å.  Such interlayer spacing dilation is highly reversible, giving rise to high rate capability (a stable capacity of 96 mA h g-1 at 1000 mA g-1) and excellent cycling performance (205 mA h g-1 after 300 cycles at 200 mA g-1).         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn ever-increasing attention for scalable elec. energy storage owing to the inexhaustible sources and wide distribution of sodium.  However, to develop feasible anode materials still remains a great challenge for the practical application of SIBs.  Here, the authors report hard carbons derived from a plentiful and deserted biomass of rice husk through a facile acid treatment and subsequent pyrolysis.  The study illustrates that the electrochem. properties of the rice husk-derived hard carbons (RHHCs) are significantly influenced by the pyrolysis temp. because of the discrepancy in their microstructure.  The RHHC pyrolyzed at 1300° (RHHC-1300) shows the highest reversible capacity of 372 mAh/g and good cycling stability and rate performance due to its large interlayer distance and suitable oxygen content.  Also, full sodium-ion batteries are assembled to examine the application prospect using Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C and RHHC-1300 as cathode and anode materials, resp., delivering a high-energy d. of 185 Wh/kg and stable cycling performance.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights on the Mechanism of Na-Ion Storage in Soft Carbon Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jian, Zelang; Bommier, Clement; Luo, Langli; Li, Zhifei; Wang, Wentao; Wang, Chongmin; Greaney, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite is the com. anode for lithium-ion batteries; however, it fails to extend its success to sodium-ion batteries.  Recently, we demonstrated that a low-cost amorphous carbon-soft carbon exhibits remarkable rate performance and stable cycling life of Na-ion storage.  However, its Na-ion storage mechanism has remained elusive, which has plagued further development of such carbon anodes.  Here, we remedy this shortfall by presenting the results from an integrated set of exptl. and computational studies that, for the first time, reveal the storage mechanism for soft carbon.  We find that sodium ions intercalate into graphenic layers, leading to an irreversible quasi-plateau at ∼0.5 V vs. Na+/Na as well as an irreversible expansion seen by in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and X-ray diffraction (XRD).  Such a high-potential plateau is correlated to the defective local structure inside the turbostratic stacking of soft carbon and the assocd. high-binding energies with Na ions, suggesting a trapping mechanism.  On the other hand, soft carbon exhibits long sloping regions above and below the quasi-plateau during the first sodiation, where the sloping regions present highly reversible behavior.  It is attributed to the more defects contained by soft carbon revealed by neutron total scattering and the assocd. pair distribution function studies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201405370"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wickramaratne, N.                         cas_cit20R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">20</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Expanded graphite as superior anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wen, Yang; He, Kai; Zhu, Yujie; Han, Fudong; Xu, Yunhua; Matsuda, Isamu; Ishii, Yoshitaka; Cumings, John; Wang, Chunsheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref21" class="refNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit21" data-doi="10.1021/nl402633n"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kushima, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Browning, N.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Probing the Failure Mechanism of SnO2 Nanowires for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Gu, Meng; Kushima, Akihiro; Shao, Yuyan; Zhang, Ji-Guang; Liu, Jun; Browning, Nigel D.; Li, Ju; Wang, Chongmin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">13</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">5203-5211</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NALEFD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1530-6984</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Non-lithium metals such as Na have attracted attention as a potential charge carrying ion for rechargeable batteries.  Using in situ TEM in combination with d. functional theory calcns., the authors probed the structural and chem. evolution of SnO2 nanowire anodes in Na-ion batteries and compared them quant. with results from Li-ion batteries.  Upon Na insertion into SnO2, a displacement reaction occurs, giving amorphous NaxSn nanoparticles dispersed in Na2O matrix.  With further Na insertion, the NaxSn crystd. into Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75).  Upon extn. of Na (de-sodiation), the NaxSn transforms to Sn nanoparticles.  Assocd. with the de-alloying, pores form, leading to a structure of Sn particles confined in a hollow matrix of Na2O.  These pores greatly increase elec. impedance, therefore accounting for the poor cyclability of SnO2.  DFT calcns. indicate that Na+ diffuses 30 times slower than Li+ in SnO2, in agreement with in situ TEM measurement.  Insertion of Na can chemomechanically soften the reaction product to a greater extent than in lithiation.  Therefore, in contrast to the lithiation of SnO2 significantly less dislocation plasticity was seen ahead of the sodiation front.  This direct comparison of the results from Na and Li highlights the crit. role of ionic size and electronic structure of different ionic species on the charge/discharge rate and failure mechanisms in these batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref22" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.06.019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Morphology-Dependent Performance of Nanostructured Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>/Ni Anode Electrodes for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>26</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">533</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">540</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

The influences of the morphol. on the performance evolution for sodium-ion batteries were studied in detail.                         cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Amorphous monodispersed hard carbon micro-spherules derived from biomass as a high performance negative electrode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yunming; Xu, Shuyin; Wu, Xiaoyan; Yu, Juezhi; Wang, Yuesheng; Hu, Yong-Sheng; Li, Hong; Chen, Liquan; Huang, Xuejie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are expected to be a promising com. alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale and low-cost elec. energy storage applications in the near future.  Despite this, the absence of a suitable neg. electrode material hinders their development.  In this contribution, the authors synthesized monodispersed hard carbon spherules (HCS) from an abundant biomass of sucrose, and studied the influence of the carbonization temp. on the microstructure and electrochem. performance.  The initial coulombic efficiency of the HCS was increased to 83% by coating its surface with soft carbon through the pyrolysis of toluene.  The plateau capacity at the low potential region increased with increasing carbonization temp.  The HCS carbonized at 1600° showed the highest plateau capacity (220 mA-h/g) and excellent cycling performance with a capacity retention of 93% after 100 cycles.  When coupled with an air-stable P2-Na2/3Ni1/3Mn2/3O2 pos. electrode, the full cell exhibited a high initial coulombic efficiency of 76%, a mean operating voltage of 3.5 V and excellent cycling performance.  The theor. energy d. of this system is 200 W-h/kg.  These promising properties are believed to be close to the level required for practical applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref24" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2014.01.132"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pol, V.                         cas_cit29R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">29</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Defect Sites-Rich Porous Carbon with Pseudocapacitive Behaviors as an Ultrafast and Long-Term Cycling Anode for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Nana; Wang, Yunxiao; Xu, Xun; Liao, Ting; Du, Yi; Bai, Zhongchao; Dou, Shixue</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">11</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9353-9361</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na-ion batteries have been regarded as promising candidates for grid-scale energy storage due to their low cost and the wide distribution of Na sources.  The main scientific challenge for their practical application is to develop suitable anodes with long-term cycling stability and high rate capacity.  Here, novel hierarchical 3-dimensional porous C materials are synthesized through an in situ template carbonization process.  Electrochem. examn. demonstrates that carbonization temp. is a key factor that affects Na+-ion-storage performance, owing to the consequent differences in surface area, pore vol., and degree of crystallinity.  The sample obtained at 600° delivers the best Na-storage performance, including long-term cycling stability (15 000 cycles) and high rate capacity (126 mAh/g at 20 A/g).  Pseudocapacitive behavior in the Na+-ion-storage process has been confirmed and studied via cyclic voltammetry.  Full cells based on the porous C anode and Na3V2(PO4)3-C cathode also deliver good cycling stability (400 cycles).  Porous C, combining the merits of high energy d. and extraordinary pseudocapacitive behavior after cycling stability, can be a promising replacement for battery/supercapacitors hybrid and suggest a design strategy for new energy-storage materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref30" class="refNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C3NR05022J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Free-Standing and Binder-Free Sodium-Ion Electrodes with Ultralong Cycle Life and High Rate Performance Based on Porous Carbon Nanofibers</span>. <i>Nanoscale</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">693</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">698</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit30R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">30</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Free-standing and binder-free sodium-ion electrodes with ultralong cycle life and high rate performance based on porous carbon nanofibers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Weihan; Zeng, Linchao; Yang, Zhenzhong; Gu, Lin; Wang, Jiaqing; Liu, Xiaowu; Cheng, Jianxiu; Yu, Yan</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanoscale</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Free-standing and binder-free porous carbon nanofibers (P-CNFs) electrodes were prepd. by pyrolysis of PAN-F127/DMF nanofibers via an electrospinning process as potential anodes for Na-ion batteries (NIB).  The P-CNFs delivers a reversible capacity of 266 mA h g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.2 C, corresponding to ∼80% of the initial charge capacity.  When cycled at a c.d. as high as 500 mA g-1 (2 C), it still delivers a reversible capacity of ∼140 mA h g-1 after 1000 cycles.  The improvement of electrochem. performance is attributed to the special design and microstructure of P-CNFs, which conferred a variety of advantages: hierarchical porous channels enabling short transport length for ions and electrons, 3D interconnected structure resulting in low contact resistances, good mech. properties leading to the excellent morphol. stability.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref31"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref31" class="refNumLink">31</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit31" data-doi="10.1039/C4EE03912B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, D.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.-B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrospun Materials for Rechargeable Batteries: From Structure Evolution to Electrochemical Performance</span>. <i>Energy Environ.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A simple and scalable electrospinning process followed by thermal treatment was used to fabricate carbon nanofibers.  The as-prepd. carbon nanofibers were investigated as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  Remarkably, due to their weakly ordered turbostratic structure and a large interlayer spacing between graphene sheets, the carbon nanofibers exhibit a dominant adsorption/insertion sodium storage mechanism that shows high reversibility.  As a result, the carbon nanofibers show excellent electrochem. performance, esp. cycle stability (97.7% capacity retention ratio over 200 cycles).  Reversible capacities of 233 and 82 mA-h/g are obtained for the carbon nanofibers at a c.d. of 0.05 A/g and even a high c.d. of 2 A/g, resp.  The excellent cycle performance, high capacity and good rate capability make the carbon nanofibers promising candidates for practical sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref33"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref33" class="refNumLink">33</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit33" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2014.08.119"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, B.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, Z.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chong, C.-B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lignin-Based Electrospun Carbon Nanofibrous Webs as Free-Standing and Binder-Free Electrodes for Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit33R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">33</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lignin-based electrospun carbon nanofibrous webs as free-standing and binder-free electrodes for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jin, Juan; Yu, Bao-jun; Shi, Zhi-qiang; Wang, Cheng-yang; Chong, Chuan-bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Low-cost and bio-based carbon nanofibrous webs (PL-CNFs) are fabricated from polyacrylonitrile (PAN)-refined lignin (RL) which is extd. from hardwood lignosulfonate via simple eletrospinning followed by stabilization and carbonization.  The effects of the PAN/RL mass ratios varying from 9/1,7/3 to 5/5 and heat-treatment temps. (HTTs) in the range from 800, 1000 to 1300 °C on morphol. and structure of PL-CNFs are systematically studied.  Due to unique morphol. and weakly ordered turbostratic microstructure of the 3-D conductive composite networks, the PL-CNFs anode obtained at 1300 °C with a mass ratio of 5/5 exhibits a high reversible capacity of 292.6 mA h g-1 with an initial efficiency of 70.5% at a const. c.d. of 0.02 A g-1 when used as free-standing and binder-free anodes for sodium ion batteries (SIBs).  The anode also presents high rate capability (210 and 80 mA h g-1 at 0.4 and 1 A g-1, resp.) and excellent cycle stability (247 mA h g-1 reversible capacity with 90.2% capacity retention ratio at 0.1 A g-1 over 200 cycles).  It is demonstrated that biomass waste lignin can be applied as a promising precursor to fabricate low-cost-high performance carbon electrode materials for SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref34"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref34" class="refNumLink">34</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit34" data-doi="10.1016/j.electacta.2014.07.079"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jin, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, Z.-Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.-Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Performance of Electrospun carbon nanofibers as free-standing and binder-free anodes for Sodium-Ion and Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Electrochim.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A series of hard carbon nanofiber-based electrodes derived from electrospun polyacrylonitrile nanofibers have been fabricated by stabilization in air at about 280° and then carbonization in N2 at heat treatment temps. between 800 and 1500°.  The electrochem. performances of the binder-free, current collector-free carbon nanofiber-based anodes in lithium-ion batteries and sodium-ion batteries are systematically investigated and compared.  We demonstrate the presence of similar alkali metal insertion mechanisms in both cases, but just the differences of the layer spacing and pore size available for lithium and sodium ion lead the discharge capacity delivered at sloping region and plateau region to vary from the kinds of alkali elements.  Although the anodes in sodium-ion batteries show poorer rate capability than that in lithium-ion batteries, they still achieve a reversible sodium intercalation capacity of 275 mA-h/g and similar cycling stability due to the conductive three-dimensional network, weakly ordered turbostratic structure and a large interlayer spacing between graphene sheets.  The feature of high capacity and stable cycling performance makes polyacrylonitrile-based carbon nanofibers to be promising candidates as electrodes in rechargeable sodium-ion batteries and lithium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref35"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref35" class="refNumLink">35</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit35" data-doi="10.1039/C4RA02009J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, K.-J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.-M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, Y.-L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">N-doped Porous Hollow Carbon Nanofibers Fabricated Using Electrospun Polymer Templates and Their Sodium Storage Properties</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">16906</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">16912</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

However, insufficient energy d. and low cycling stability of current sodium-ion batteries hinder their practical use for next-generation smart power grid and stationary storage applications.  Electrospun carbon microfibers have recently been introduced as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  However, electrospinning is not feasible for mass prodn. of carbon microfibers due to its complex processing condition, low prodn. rate and high cost.  Herein, we report centrifugal spinning, a high-rate and low-cost microfiber prodn. method, as an alternative approach to electrospinning for carbon microfiber prodn. and introduce centrifugally-spun carbon microfibers (CMFs) and porous carbon microfibers (PCMFs) as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.                         cas_cit42R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">42</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Graphene highly scattered in porous carbon nanofibers: a binder-free and high-performance anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Yongchang; Fan, Li-Zhen; Jiao, Lifang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphene monolayers or bilayers highly scattered in porous C nanofibers (denoted as G/C) are 1st prepd. by a feasible electrospinning technique.  Meanwhile, G/C with the character of a flexible membrane adherent on Cu foil is directly used as binder-free anode for Na-ion batteries, exhibiting fascinating electrochem. performance in terms of high reversible capacity (432.3 mAh/g at 100 mA/g), exceptional rate capability (261.1 mAh/g even at 10 000 mA/g), and ultra-long cycling life (91% capacity retention after 1000 cycles).  This is due to the synergistic effect between the highly exfoliated graphene layers and the porous C nanofibers, which can provide massive active Na-storage sites, ensure sufficient electrolyte infiltration, offer open ionic diffusion channels and oriented electronic transfer pathways, and prevent graphene agglomeration as well as C nanofiber fracture upon prolonged cycling.  The findings shed new insights into the quest for high-performance C-based anode materials of Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref43" class="refNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit43" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502217"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Reduced graphene oxide, an intensively investigated material for Li-ion batteries, has shown mostly unsatisfactory performance in Na-ion batteries, since its d-spacing is believed to be too small for effective insertion/deinsertion of Na+ ions.  Herein, a facile method was developed to produce boron-functionalized reduced graphene oxide (BF-rGO), with an enlarged interlayer spacing and defect-rich structure, which effectively accommodates the sodiation/desodiation and provides more active sites.  The Na/BF-rGO half cells exhibit unprecedented long cycling stability, with ∼89.4% capacity retained after 5000 cycles (0.002% capacity decay per cycle) at 1000 mA·g-1 c.d.                         cas_cit45R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">45</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Fluorine-Doped Carbon Particles Derived from Lotus Petioles as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Pengzi; Qiao, Bin; Du, Yichen; Li, Yafei; Zhou, Xiaosi; Dai, Zhihui; Bao, Jianchun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry C</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In contrast to the extensive study of the electrochem. performance of conventional C materials in Na-ion batteries, there was scarcely any study of Na storage property of F-doped C.  Here the authors report for the 1st time the application of F-doped C particles (F-CP) synthesized through pyrolysis of lotus petioles as anode materials for Na-ion batteries.  Electrochem. tests demonstrate that the F-CP electrode delivers an initial charge capacity of 230 mA h g-1 at a c.d. of 50 mA g-1 between 0.001 and 2.8 V, which greatly outperforms the corresponding value of 149 mA h g-1 for the counterpart banana peels-derived C (BPC).  Even under 200 mA g-1, the F-CP electrode could still exhibit a charge capacity of 228 mA h g-1 with initial charge capacity retention of 99.1% after 200 cycles compared to the BPC electrode with 107 mA h g-1 and 71.8%.  The F-doping and the large interlayer distance as well as the disorder structure contribute to a lowering of the Na ion insertion-extn. barrier, thus promoting the Na+ diffusion and providing more active sites for Na+ storage.  In specific, the F-CP electrode shows longer low-discharge-plateau and better kinetics than does the common C-based electrode.  The unique electrochem. performance of F-CP enriches the existing knowledge of the C-based electrode materials and broadens avenues for rational design of anode materials in Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref46"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref46" class="refNumLink">46</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit46" data-doi="10.1002/advs.201600243"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zou, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ji, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Large-Area Carbon Nanosheets Doped with Phosphorus: A High-Performance Anode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. This is the first time to realize the conversion from 0D CDs to 2D nanosheets doped with phosphorus.  The sodium storage behavior of phosphorus-doped carbon material is also investigated for the first time.  As anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), P-CNSs exhibit superb performances for electrochemical storage of sodium.                         cas_cit48R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">48</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">"Protrusions" or "holes" in graphene: which is the better choice for sodium ion storage?</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Yijun; Tang, Dai-Ming; Zhang, Chao; Zhang, Yihui; Liang, Qifeng; Chen, Shimou; Weng, Qunhong; Zhou, Min; Xue, Yanming; Liu, Jiangwei; Wu, Jinghua; Cui, Qiu Hong; Lian, Chao; Hou, Guolin; Yuan, Fangli; Bando, Yoshio; Golberg, Dmitri; Wang, Xi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">979-986</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The main challenge assocd. with sodium-ion battery (SIB) anodes is a search for novel candidate materials with high capacity and excellent rate capability.  The most commonly used and effective route for graphene-based anode design is the introduction of in-plane "hole" defects via nitrogen-doping; this creates a spacious reservoir for storing more energy.  Inspired by mountains in nature, herein, we propose another way - the introduction of blistering in graphene instead of making "holes"; this facilitates adsorbing/inserting more Na+ ions.  In order to properly answer the key question: ""protrusions" or "holes" in graphene, which is better for sodium ion storage", two types of anode materials with a similar doping level were designed: a phosphorus-doped graphene (GP, with protrusions) and a nitrogen-doped graphene (GN, with holes).  As compared with GN, the GP anode perfectly satisfies all the desired criteria: it reveals an ultrahigh capacity (374 mA h g-1 after 120 cycles at 25 mA g-1) comparable to the best graphite anodes in a std. Li-ion battery (∼372 mA h g-1), and exhibits an excellent rate capability (210 mA h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).  In situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) expts. and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. were utilized to uncover the origin of the enhanced electrochem. activity of "protrusions" compared to "holes" in SIBs, down to the at. scale.  The introduction of protrusions through P-doping into graphene is envisaged to be a novel effective way to enhance the capacity and rate performance of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref49" class="refNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit49" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00172"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Surta, T.                         cas_cit49R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">49</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High Capacity of Hard Carbon Anode in Na-Ion Batteries Unlocked by POx Doping</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Zhifei; Ma, Lu; Surta, Todd Wesley; Bommier, Clement; Jian, Zelang; Xing, Zhenyu; Stickle, William F.; Dolgos, Michelle; Amine, Khalil; Lu, Jun; Wu, Tianpin; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Energy Letters</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The capacity of hard carbon anodes in Na-ion batteries rarely reaches values beyond 300 mAh/g.  It is reported that doping POx into local structures of hard carbon increases its reversible capacity from 283-359 mAh/g.  It is confirmed that the doped POx is redox inactive by X-ray adsorption near edge structure measurements, thus not contributing to the higher capacity.  Two significant changes are obsd. of hard carbon's local structures caused by doping.  First, the (002) d-spacing inside the turbostratic nanodomains is increased, revealed by both lab. and synchrotron X-ray diffraction.  Second, doping turns turbostratic nanodomains more defective along ab planes, indicated by neutron total scattering and the assocd. pair distribution function studies.  The local structural changes of hard carbon are correlated to the higher capacity, where both the plateau and slope regions in the potential profiles are enhanced.  The study demonstrates that Na-ion storage in hard carbon heavily depends on carbon local structures, where such structures, despite being disordered, can be tuned toward unusually high capacities.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref50" class="refNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit50" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b01986"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lü, H.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Samples of lignocellulosic material, stem of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera), were carbonized at different temps. True densities of the products generally increased with increase in carbonization temp.  Impregnated samples (acid/base) showed wider differences in densities at 400 (1.978/1.375 g/cm3) than at 600° (1.955/2.010 g/cm3).  Without impregnation, the sample carbonized at 600° showed higher d. of 2.190 g/cm3.  This sample has impervious surface with BET surface area of 124 m2/g.  Acid-impregnated sample carbonized at 500° has the highest surface area of 1100 m2/g and most regular pores as evidenced by SEM micrographs.  The amts. of oil adsorbed decreased with increase in carbonization temp.  Without impregnation, sample carbonized at 400° exhibited equil. adsorption of 4 g/g which decreases to about a half for sample carbonized at 600°.  Impregnation led to different adsorptive capacities.  There are resp. increase (48 wt.%) and decrease (5 wt.%) by the acid- or base-impregnated samples carbonized at 600°.             (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are considered a top alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of sodium-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.  However, the absence of a suitable neg. electrode material hinders their development.  In this contribution, we report the prepn. of nitrogen and phosphorous co-doped carbon microspheres through a hydrothermal process followed by heat treatment in presence of (NH4)2HPO4 and investigate their electrochem. performance as anode in sodium-ion batteries.  Benefiting from the N, P co-doping, the obtained N, P co-doped carbon microspheres demonstrates high specific capacity, excellent cycle stability as well as good rate performance.  The as prepd. N, P co-doped carbon microspheres can deliver a reversible capacity of 305 mAh/g at a c.d. of 0.1 A/g and even deliver a capacity of 136 mAh/g at 5 A/g.  This superior sodium storage performance of N, P co-doped carbon makes it as a promising low-cost anode candidate for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref53" class="refNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit53" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700403"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L.                         "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref55" class="refNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit55" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Murata, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishikawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozeki, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogata, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gotoh, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujiwara, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv.
