Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3859</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3867</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.201100854</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D21%26publication_year%3D2011%26pages%3D3859-3867%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct.%2BMater.%26author%3DS.%2BKomaba%26author%3DW.%2BMurata%26author%3DT.%2BIshikawa%26author%3DN.%2BYabuuchi%26author%3DT.%2BOzeki%26author%3DT.%2BNakayama%26author%3DA.%2BOgata%26author%3DK.%2BGotoh%26author%3DK.%2BFujiwara%26title%3DElectrochemical%2BNa%2BInsertion%2Band%2BSolid%2BElectrolyte%2BInterphase%2Bfor%2BHard-Carbon%2BElectrodes%2Band%2BApplication%2Bto%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201100854&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b05618&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201100854&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Murata, Wataru; Ishikawa, Toru; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Nakayama, Tetsuri; Ogata, Atsushi; Gotoh, Kazuma; Fujiwara, Kazuya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3859-3867</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref56" class="refNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit56" data-doi="10.1007/s40553-014-0041-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, T.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, M.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, C.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium-Ion Insertion Kinetics of Na-Doped LiFePO<sub>4</sub> as Cathode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Metall. Mater. Trans. E</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">33</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">38</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s40553-014-0041-6</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D2%26publication_year%3D2015%26pages%3D33-38%26journal%3DMetall.%2BMater.%2BTrans.%2BE%26author%3DY.-R.%2BZhu%26author%3DR.%2BZhang%26author%3DL.%2BDeng%26author%3DT.-F.%2BYi%26author%3DM.-F.%2BYe%26author%3DJ.-H.%2BYao%26author%3DC.-S.%2BDai%26title%3DLithium-Ion%2BInsertion%2BKinetics%2Bof%2BNa-Doped%2BLiFePO4%2Bas%2BCathode%2BMaterials%2Bfor%2BLithium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1007%252Fs40553-014-0041-6&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.8b05618&amp;doiOfLink=10.1007%2Fs40553-014-0041-6&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-Ion Insertion Kinetics of Na-Doped LiFePO4 as Cathode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yan-Rong; Zhang, Rui; Deng, Li; Yi, Ting-Feng; Ye, Ming-Fu; Yao, Jin-Han; Dai, Chang-Song</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Metallurgical and Materials Transactions E</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">33-38</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MMTECL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2196-2944</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-doped Li1-xNaxFePO4 (x = 0, 0.05, 0.1, and 0.2) materials were synthesized by a simple high-temp. solid-state method.  The structure, morphol., and kinetic performances of the samples were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), scanning electron microscope (SEM), cyclic voltammetry (CV), and electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS).  XRD indicates that all samples are in accordance with the std. LiFePO4 with olivine structure.  SEM reveals that the particle size of all samples is about 1 to 2 μm.  CV exhibits that Na doping obviously improves the reversibility and dynamic behaviors of lithium intercalation and deintercalation.  EIS shows that Na doping decreases the charge transfer resistance of LiFePO4 and improves the lithium diffusion coeffs.  It can be concluded that Na doping results in lower electrode polarization and higher lithium-ion diffusion coeff., which can effectively improve the kinetic performance of LiFePO4.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></div><script type="text/javascript" nonce="931888d23c4954b8-SJC">window.refViewer={'ref1':['cit1'],'ref2':['cit2'],'ref3':['cit3'],'ref4':['cit4'],'ref5':['cit5'],'ref6':['cit6'],'ref7':['cit7'],'ref8':['cit8'],'ref9':['cit9'],'ref10':['cit10'],'ref11':['cit11'],'ref12':['cit12'],'ref13':['cit13'],'ref14':['cit14'],'ref15':['cit15'],'ref16':['cit16'],'ref17':['cit17'],'ref18':['cit18'],'ref19':['cit19'],'ref20':['cit20'],'ref21':['cit21'],'ref22':['cit22'],'ref23':['cit23'],'ref24':['cit24'],'ref25':['cit25'],'ref26':['cit26'],'ref27':['cit27'],'ref28':['cit28'],'ref29':['cit29'],'ref30':['cit30'],'ref31':['cit31'],'ref32':['cit32'],'ref33':['cit33'],'ref34':['cit34'],'ref35':['cit35'],'ref36':['cit36'],'ref37':['cit37'],'ref38':['cit38'],'ref39':['cit39'],'ref40':['cit40'],'ref41':['cit41'],'ref42':['cit42'],'ref43':['cit43'],'ref44':['cit44'],'ref45':['cit45'],'ref46':['cit46'],'ref47':['cit47'],'ref48':['cit48'],'ref49':['cit49'],'ref50':['cit50'],'ref51':['cit51'],'ref52':['cit52'],'ref53':['cit53'],'ref54':['cit54'],'ref55':['cit55'],'ref56':['cit56']}</script><div class="articleCitedByDropzone" data-pb-dropzone="articleCitedByDropzone"><div class="cited-by"><div class="article_content-header cited-content_header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div tabindex="0" class="article_content-title"><h2 id="citeThis">Cited By</h2><div class="article__copy"> <button class="article__copy__btn"><i aria-hidden="true" class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><div class="cited-by__content"><div class="sciteMetricsDropzone">

Soc.                         rightTab-cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref3" class="rightTabRefNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit3" data-doi="10.1021/cr500192f"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kubota, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dahbi, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Research Development on Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Prussian blue analogs consisting of different transition-metal ions (Fe, Mn, Ni, Cu, Co and Zn) have been synthesized at room temp.                         rightTab-cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Na2FeP2O7 as a Promising Iron-Based Pyrophosphate Cathode for Sodium Rechargeable Batteries: A Combined Experimental and Theoretical Study</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Heejin; Shakoor, R. A.; Park, Chansun; Lim, Soo Yeon; Kim, Joo-Seong; Jo, Yong Nam; Cho, Woosuk; Miyasaka, Keiichi; Kahraman, Ramazan; Jung, Yousung; Choi, Jang Wook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">9</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1147-1155</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Considering the promising electrochem. performance of the recently reported pyrophosphate family in Li ion batteries as well as the increasing importance of Na ion batteries (SIBs) for emerging large-scale applications, here, the crystal structure, electrochem. properties, and thermal stability of Na2FeP2O7, the 1st example ever reported in the pyrophosphate family for SIBs, are studied.  Na2FeP2O7 maintains well-defined channel structures (triclinic framework under the P1 space group) and exhibits a reversible capacity of ≈90 mA-h g-1 with good cycling performance.  Both quasi-equil. measurements and 1st-principles calcns. consistently indicate that Na2FeP2O7 undergoes 2 kinds of reactions over the entire voltage range of 2.0-4.5 V (vs. Na/Na+): a single-phase reaction around 2.5 V and 2-phase reactions in the voltage range of 3.0-3.25 V.  Na2FeP2O7 shows excellent thermal stability up to 500°, even in the partially de-sodiated state (NaFeP2O7), which suggests its safe character, a property that is very crit. for large-scale battery applications.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref7" class="rightTabRefNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit7" data-doi="10.1038/ncomms5358"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barpanda, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Oyama, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nishimura, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chung, S.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Rechargeable lithium batteries have ushered the wireless revolution over last two decades and are now matured to enable green automobiles.  However, the growing concern on scarcity and large-scale applications of lithium resources have steered effort to realize sustainable sodium-ion batteries, Na and Fe being abundant and low-cost charge carrier and redox center, resp.  However, their performance is limited owing to low operating voltage and sluggish kinetics.  Here we report a hitherto-unknown material with entirely new compn. and structure with the first alluaudite-type sulfate framework, Na2Fe2(SO4)3, registering the highest-ever Fe3+/Fe2+ redox potential at 3.8 V (vs. Na, and hence 4.1 V vs. Li) along with fast rate kinetics.     </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries, representative members of the post-lithium-battery club, are very attractive and promising for large-scale energy storage applications.  The increasing technological improvements in sodium-ion batteries (Na-ion batteries) are being driven by the demand for Na-based electrode materials that are resource-abundant, cost-effective, and long lasting.  Polyanion-type compounds are among the most promising electrode materials for Na-ion batteries due to their stability, safety, and suitable operating voltages.  The most representative polyanion-type electrode materials are Na3V2(PO4)3 and NaTi2(PO4)3 for Na-based cathode and anode materials, respectively.  Both show superior electrochemical properties and attractive prospects in terms of their development and application in Na-ion batteries.  Carbonophosphate Na3MnCO3PO4 and amorphous FePO4 have also recently emerged and are contributing to further developing the research scope of polyanion-type Na-ion batteries.  However, the typical low conductivity and relatively low capacity performance of such materials still restrict their development.  This paper presents a brief review of the research progress of polyanion-type electrode materials for Na-ion batteries, summarizing recent accomplishments, highlighting emerging strategies, and discussing the remaining challenges of such systems.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref9" class="rightTabRefNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit9" data-doi="10.1149/1.1393348"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Stevens, D. However, it is still unclear whether carbon anodes can realize large-scale com. application in SIBs as in lithium-ion batteries.  Recently, great attention has been devoted to hard carbon which has been treated as a promising choice.  Herein, we observe that the turbostratic lattice of kelp-derived hard carbon (KHC) is repeatedly expandable and shrinkable upon cycling, where the interlayer distance varies between 3.9 and 4.3 Å.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have drawn ever-increasing attention for scalable elec. energy storage owing to the inexhaustible sources and wide distribution of sodium.  However, to develop feasible anode materials still remains a great challenge for the practical application of SIBs.  Here, the authors report hard carbons derived from a plentiful and deserted biomass of rice husk through a facile acid treatment and subsequent pyrolysis.  The study illustrates that the electrochem. properties of the rice husk-derived hard carbons (RHHCs) are significantly influenced by the pyrolysis temp. because of the discrepancy in their microstructure.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A pitch-derived soft carbon is successfully prepd. by easy-accessible pyrolysis process in this work.  The electrochem. performance of the acquired soft carbon can be improved via doping phosphorus.                         rightTab-cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights on the Mechanism of Na-Ion Storage in Soft Carbon Anode</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jian, Zelang; Bommier, Clement; Luo, Langli; Li, Zhifei; Wang, Wentao; Wang, Chongmin; Greaney, P. Alex; Ji, Xiulei</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite is the com. anode for lithium-ion batteries; however, it fails to extend its success to sodium-ion batteries.  Recently, we demonstrated that a low-cost amorphous carbon-soft carbon exhibits remarkable rate performance and stable cycling life of Na-ion storage.  However, its Na-ion storage mechanism has remained elusive, which has plagued further development of such carbon anodes.  Here, we remedy this shortfall by presenting the results from an integrated set of exptl. and computational studies that, for the first time, reveal the storage mechanism for soft carbon.  We find that sodium ions intercalate into graphenic layers, leading to an irreversible quasi-plateau at ∼0.5 V vs. Na+/Na as well as an irreversible expansion seen by in situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) and X-ray diffraction (XRD).  Such a high-potential plateau is correlated to the defective local structure inside the turbostratic stacking of soft carbon and the assocd. high-binding energies with Na ions, suggesting a trapping mechanism.  On the other hand, soft carbon exhibits long sloping regions above and below the quasi-plateau during the first sodiation, where the sloping regions present highly reversible behavior.  It is attributed to the more defects contained by soft carbon revealed by neutron total scattering and the assocd. pair distribution function studies.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref18" class="rightTabRefNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit18" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201405370"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wickramaratne, N.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Graphite, as the most common anode for com. Li-ion batteries, has been reported to have a very low capacity when used as a Na-ion battery anode.  It is well known that electrochem. insertion of Na+ into graphite is significantly hindered by the insufficient interlayer spacing.  Here we report expanded graphite as a Na-ion battery anode.  Prepd. through a process of oxidn. and partial redn. on graphite, expanded graphite has an enlarged interlayer lattice distance of 4.3 Å yet retains an analogous long-range-ordered layered structure to graphite.  In situ transmission electron microscopy has demonstrated that the Na-ion can be reversibly inserted into and extd. from expanded graphite.  Galvanostatic studies show that expanded graphite can deliver a high reversible capacity of 284 mAh g-1 at a c.d. of 20 mA g-1, maintain a capacity of 184 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, and retain 73.92% of its capacity after 2,000 cycles.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref21"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref21" class="rightTabRefNumLink">21</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit21" data-doi="10.1021/nl402633n"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kushima, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.-G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Browning, N.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Non-lithium metals such as Na have attracted attention as a potential charge carrying ion for rechargeable batteries.  Using in situ TEM in combination with d. functional theory calcns., the authors probed the structural and chem. evolution of SnO2 nanowire anodes in Na-ion batteries and compared them quant. with results from Li-ion batteries.  Upon Na insertion into SnO2, a displacement reaction occurs, giving amorphous NaxSn nanoparticles dispersed in Na2O matrix.  With further Na insertion, the NaxSn crystd. into Na15Sn4 (x = 3.75).  Upon extn. of Na (de-sodiation), the NaxSn transforms to Sn nanoparticles.  Assocd. with the de-alloying, pores form, leading to a structure of Sn particles confined in a hollow matrix of Na2O.  These pores greatly increase elec. impedance, therefore accounting for the poor cyclability of SnO2.  DFT calcns. indicate that Na+ diffuses 30 times slower than Li+ in SnO2, in agreement with in situ TEM measurement.  Insertion of Na can chemomechanically soften the reaction product to a greater extent than in lithiation.  Therefore, in contrast to the lithiation of SnO2 significantly less dislocation plasticity was seen ahead of the sodiation front.  This direct comparison of the results from Na and Li highlights the crit. role of ionic size and electronic structure of different ionic species on the charge/discharge rate and failure mechanisms in these batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref22" class="rightTabRefNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit22" data-doi="10.1016/j.nanoen.2016.06.019"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Song, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bai, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Morphology-Dependent Performance of Nanostructured Ni<sub>3</sub>S<sub>2</sub>/Ni Anode Electrodes for High Performance Sodium Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Nano Energy</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>26</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">533</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">540</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are expected to be a promising com. alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for large-scale and low-cost elec. energy storage applications in the near future.  Despite this, the absence of a suitable neg. electrode material hinders their development.  In this contribution, the authors synthesized monodispersed hard carbon spherules (HCS) from an abundant biomass of sucrose, and studied the influence of the carbonization temp. on the microstructure and electrochem. performance.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Room-temp. Na-ion batteries have been regarded as promising candidates for grid-scale energy storage due to their low cost and the wide distribution of Na sources.  The main scientific challenge for their practical application is to develop suitable anodes with long-term cycling stability and high rate capacity.  Here, novel hierarchical 3-dimensional porous C materials are synthesized through an in situ template carbonization process.  Electrochem. examn. demonstrates that carbonization temp. is a key factor that affects Na+-ion-storage performance, owing to the consequent differences in surface area, pore vol., and degree of crystallinity.  The sample obtained at 600° delivers the best Na-storage performance, including long-term cycling stability (15 000 cycles) and high rate capacity (126 mAh/g at 20 A/g).  Pseudocapacitive behavior in the Na+-ion-storage process has been confirmed and studied via cyclic voltammetry.  Full cells based on the porous C anode and Na3V2(PO4)3-C cathode also deliver good cycling stability (400 cycles).  Porous C, combining the merits of high energy d. and extraordinary pseudocapacitive behavior after cycling stability, can be a promising replacement for battery/supercapacitors hybrid and suggest a design strategy for new energy-storage materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref30"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref30" class="rightTabRefNumLink">30</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit30" data-doi="10.1039/C3NR05022J"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zeng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Free-Standing and Binder-Free Sodium-Ion Electrodes with Ultralong Cycle Life and High Rate Performance Based on Porous Carbon Nanofibers</span>. <i>Nanoscale</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">693</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">698</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

Electrospun carbon microfibers have recently been introduced as a high-performance anode material for sodium-ion batteries.  However, electrospinning is not feasible for mass prodn. of carbon microfibers due to its complex processing condition, low prodn. rate and high cost.  Herein, we report centrifugal spinning, a high-rate and low-cost microfiber prodn. method, as an alternative approach to electrospinning for carbon microfiber prodn. and introduce centrifugally-spun carbon microfibers (CMFs) and porous carbon microfibers (PCMFs) as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. Meanwhile, G/C with the character of a flexible membrane adherent on Cu foil is directly used as binder-free anode for Na-ion batteries, exhibiting fascinating electrochem. performance in terms of high reversible capacity (432.3 mAh/g at 100 mA/g), exceptional rate capability (261.1 mAh/g even at 10 000 mA/g), and ultra-long cycling life (91% capacity retention after 1000 cycles). The findings shed new insights into the quest for high-performance C-based anode materials of Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref43"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref43" class="rightTabRefNumLink">43</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit43" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502217"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Reduced graphene oxide, an intensively investigated material for Li-ion batteries, has shown mostly unsatisfactory performance in Na-ion batteries, since its d-spacing is believed to be too small for effective insertion/deinsertion of Na+ ions.         </div><div class="casAbstract">In contrast to the extensive study of the electrochem. performance of conventional C materials in Na-ion batteries, there was scarcely any study of Na storage property of F-doped C. In specific, the F-CP electrode shows longer low-discharge-plateau and better kinetics than does the common C-based electrode.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The main challenge assocd. with sodium-ion battery (SIB) anodes is a search for novel candidate materials with high capacity and excellent rate capability.  The most commonly used and effective route for graphene-based anode design is the introduction of in-plane "hole" defects via nitrogen-doping; this creates a spacious reservoir for storing more energy.  Inspired by mountains in nature, herein, we propose another way - the introduction of blistering in graphene instead of making "holes"; this facilitates adsorbing/inserting more Na+ ions.  In order to properly answer the key question: ""protrusions" or "holes" in graphene, which is better for sodium ion storage", two types of anode materials with a similar doping level were designed: a phosphorus-doped graphene (GP, with protrusions) and a nitrogen-doped graphene (GN, with holes).  As compared with GN, the GP anode perfectly satisfies all the desired criteria: it reveals an ultrahigh capacity (374 mA h g-1 after 120 cycles at 25 mA g-1) comparable to the best graphite anodes in a std. Li-ion battery (∼372 mA h g-1), and exhibits an excellent rate capability (210 mA h g-1 at 500 mA g-1).  In situ transmission electron microscopy (TEM) expts. and d. functional theory (DFT) calcns. were utilized to uncover the origin of the enhanced electrochem. activity of "protrusions" compared to "holes" in SIBs, down to the at. scale.  The introduction of protrusions through P-doping into graphene is envisaged to be a novel effective way to enhance the capacity and rate performance of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref49"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref49" class="rightTabRefNumLink">49</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit49" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.6b00172"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Surta, T.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The capacity of hard carbon anodes in Na-ion batteries rarely reaches values beyond 300 mAh/g.  It is reported that doping POx into local structures of hard carbon increases its reversible capacity from 283-359 mAh/g.  It is confirmed that the doped POx is redox inactive by X-ray adsorption near edge structure measurements, thus not contributing to the higher capacity.  Two significant changes are obsd. of hard carbon's local structures caused by doping.  First, the (002) d-spacing inside the turbostratic nanodomains is increased, revealed by both lab. and synchrotron X-ray diffraction.  Second, doping turns turbostratic nanodomains more defective along ab planes, indicated by neutron total scattering and the assocd. pair distribution function studies.  The local structural changes of hard carbon are correlated to the higher capacity, where both the plateau and slope regions in the potential profiles are enhanced.  The study demonstrates that Na-ion storage in hard carbon heavily depends on carbon local structures, where such structures, despite being disordered, can be tuned toward unusually high capacities.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref50"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref50" class="rightTabRefNumLink">50</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit50" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.7b01986"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lü, H.-Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.     <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12518-12527</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1944-8244</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, a flexible and self-supporting P-doped carbon cloth (FPCC), which is composed of interwoven mesh of hollow microtubules with porous carbon walls, is prepd. via a vacuum-sealed doping technol. by employing the com. available cotton cloth as sustainable and scalable raw material.  When directly used as binder-free anode for sodium-ion batteries, the as-prepd. FPCC delivers superior Na-storage properties in terms of specific capacity up to 242.4 mA h g-1, high initial Coulombic efficiency of ∼72%, excellent rate capabilities (e.g., 123.1 mA h g-1 at a high current of 1 A g-1), and long-term cycle life (e.g., ∼88% capacity retention after even 600 cycles).  All these electrochem. data are better than the undoped carbon cloth control, demonstrating the significance of P-doping to enhance the Na-storage properties of cotton-derived carbon anode.  Furthermore, the technologies of electrochem. impedance spectroscopy and galvanostatic intermittent titrn. technique are implemented to disclose the decrease of charge transfer resistance and improvement of Na-migration kinetics, resp.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref51"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref51" class="rightTabRefNumLink">51</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit51" data-doi="10.1016/j.jaap.2008.07.003"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jibril, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Houache, O.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Al-Maamari, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Al-Rashidi, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Effects of H<sub>3</sub>PO<sub>4</sub> and KOH in Carbonization of Lignocellulosic Material</span>. <i>J. Anal. Appl. Pyrolysis</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2008</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>83</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">151</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">156</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.jaap.2008.07.003</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit51R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">51</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Effects of H3PO4 and KOH in carbonization of lignocellulosic material</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Jibril, Baba; Houache, Omar; Al-Maamari, Rashid; Al-Rashidi, Badir</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Analytical and Applied Pyrolysis</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2008</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">83</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">151-156</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JAAPDD</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0165-2370</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Samples of lignocellulosic material, stem of date palm (Phoenix dactylifera), were carbonized at different temps. (400-600°) to investigate the effects of their impregnation with aq. soln. of either phosphoric acid (85 wt.%) or potassium hydroxide (3 wt.%).  The products were characterized using BET nitrogen adsorption, helium pycnometry, SEM and oil adsorption from oil-water emulsion (oil viscosity, 60 mPa s at 25°).  True densities of the products generally increased with increase in carbonization temp.  Impregnated samples (acid/base) showed wider differences in densities at 400 (1.978/1.375 g/cm3) than at 600° (1.955/2.010 g/cm3).  Without impregnation, the sample carbonized at 600° showed higher d. of 2.190 g/cm3.  This sample has impervious surface with BET surface area of 124 m2/g.  Acid-impregnated sample carbonized at 500° has the highest surface area of 1100 m2/g and most regular pores as evidenced by SEM micrographs.  The amts. of oil adsorbed decreased with increase in carbonization temp.  Without impregnation, sample carbonized at 400° exhibited equil. adsorption of 4 g/g which decreases to about a half for sample carbonized at 600°.  Impregnation led to different adsorptive capacities.  There are resp. increase (48 wt.%) and decrease (5 wt.%) by the acid- or base-impregnated samples carbonized at 600°.  This suggests higher occurrence of oil adsorption-enhancing surface functional groups such as carbonyl, carboxyl and phenolic in the former sample.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref52"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref52" class="rightTabRefNumLink">52</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit52" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2015.12.066"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Peng, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Srinivasan, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ramakrishna, S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Preparation of Nitrogen and Phosphorous Co-Doped Carbon Microspheres and Their Superior Performance as Anode in Sodium-ion Batteries</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2016</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>99</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">556</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">563</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1016/j.carbon.2015.12.066</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit52R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">52</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Preparation of nitrogen- and phosphorus co-doped carbon microspheres and their superior performance as anode in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Yueming; Wang, Zhiguang; Li, Linlin; Peng, Shengjie; Zhang, Long; Srinivasan, Madhavi; Ramakrishna, Seeram</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">99</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">556-563</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0008-6223</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are considered a top alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale renewable energy storage units due to their low cost and the abundance of sodium-bearing precursors in the earth's mineral deposits.  However, the absence of a suitable neg. electrode material hinders their development.  In this contribution, we report the prepn. of nitrogen and phosphorous co-doped carbon microspheres through a hydrothermal process followed by heat treatment in presence of (NH4)2HPO4 and investigate their electrochem. performance as anode in sodium-ion batteries.  Benefiting from the N, P co-doping, the obtained N, P co-doped carbon microspheres demonstrates high specific capacity, excellent cycle stability as well as good rate performance.  The as prepd. N, P co-doped carbon microspheres can deliver a reversible capacity of 305 mAh/g at a c.d. of 0.1 A/g and even deliver a capacity of 136 mAh/g at 5 A/g.  This superior sodium storage performance of N, P co-doped carbon makes it as a promising low-cost anode candidate for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref53"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref53" class="rightTabRefNumLink">53</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit53" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201700403"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, L. F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sushko, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, K. S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shao, Y. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, M. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, X. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L. Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Feng, J. W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, Y. L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ai, X. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, H. X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Manipulating Adsorption–Insertion Mechanisms in Nanostructured Carbon Materials for High-Efficiency Sodium Ion Storage</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2017</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>7</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1700403</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201700403</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref54"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref54" class="rightTabRefNumLink">54</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit54" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201702781"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, Y. C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Z. H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L. M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Amine, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Insights into the Na<sup>+</sup> Storage Mechanism of Phosphorus-Functionalized Hard Carbon as Ultrahigh Capacity Anodes</span>. <i>Adv. Energy Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1702781</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/aenm.201702781</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord empty" id="
                            rightTab-cas_R
                        "><div class="casContent">There is no corresponding record for this reference.</div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref55"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref55" class="rightTabRefNumLink">55</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit55" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201100854"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Murata, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ishikawa, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ozeki, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Nakayama, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogata, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gotoh, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fujiwara, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Funct. Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2011</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>21</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3859</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3867</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.201100854</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit55R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">55</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Na Insertion and Solid Electrolyte Interphase for Hard-Carbon Electrodes and Application to Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Murata, Wataru; Ishikawa, Toru; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ozeki, Tomoaki; Nakayama, Tetsuri; Ogata, Atsushi; Gotoh, Kazuma; Fujiwara, Kazuya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2011</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">21</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">20</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3859-3867</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1616-301X</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Recently, lithium-ion batteries were attracting more interest for use in automotive applications.  Lithium resources are confirmed to be unevenly distributed in South America, and the cost of the lithium raw materials has roughly doubled from the first practical application in 1991 to the present and is increasing due to global demand for lithium-ion accumulators.  Since the electrochem. equiv. and std. potential of sodium are the most advantageous after lithium, sodium based energy storage is of great interest to realize lithium-free high energy and high voltage batteries.  However, to the best of authors knowledge, there were no successful reports on electrochem. sodium insertion materials for battery applications; the major challenge is the neg. electrode and its passivation.  In this study, high capacity and excellent reversibility are achieved of sodium-insertion performance of hard-carbon and layered NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 electrodes in propylene carbonate electrolyte solns.  The structural change and passivation for hard-carbon are investigated to study the reversible sodium insertion.  The 3-V secondary Na-ion battery possessing environmental and cost friendliness, Na+-shuttlecock hard-carbon/NaNi0.5Mn0.5O2 cell, demonstrates steady cycling performance as next generation secondary batteries and an alternative to Li-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="rightTab-ref56"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="#ref56" class="rightTabRefNumLink">56</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="rightTab-cit56" data-doi="10.1007/s40553-014-0041-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.-R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Deng, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yi, T.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ye, M.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yao, J.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dai, C.-S.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lithium-Ion Insertion Kinetics of Na-Doped LiFePO<sub>4</sub> as Cathode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Metall. Mater. Trans. E</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2015</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>2</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">33</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">38</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1007/s40553-014-0041-6</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><div class="casRecord" id="
                        rightTab-cas_cit56R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">56</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lithium-Ion Insertion Kinetics of Na-Doped LiFePO4 as Cathode Materials for Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhu, Yan-Rong; Zhang, Rui; Deng, Li; Yi, Ting-Feng; Ye, Ming-Fu; Yao, Jin-Han; Dai, Chang-Song</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Metallurgical and Materials Transactions E</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2015</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">2</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">33-38</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">MMTECL</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2196-2944</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Springer</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-doped Li1-xNaxFePO4 (x = 0, 0.05, 0.1, and 0.2) materials were synthesized by a simple high-temp. solid-state method.  The structure, morphol., and kinetic performances of the samples were characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD), scanning electron microscope (SEM), cyclic voltammetry (CV), and electrochem. impedance spectroscopy (EIS).  XRD indicates that all samples are in accordance with the std. LiFePO4 with olivine structure.  SEM reveals that the particle size of all samples is about 1 to 2 μm.  CV exhibits that Na doping obviously improves the reversibility and dynamic behaviors of lithium intercalation and deintercalation.  EIS shows that Na doping decreases the charge transfer resistance of LiFePO4 and improves the lithium diffusion coeffs.  It can be concluded that Na doping results in lower electrode polarization and higher lithium-ion diffusion coeff., which can effectively improve the kinetic performance of LiFePO4.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li></ol></li></ul></div></div></div></div></article><div class="w-slide article__w-slide">

                <a title="Abstract" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#Abstract" data-id="article_stickyheader_JumpTo_Abstract">
                   <span>Abstract</span>

            <li>
                <a title="1.  Introduction" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#_i2" data-id="article_stickyheader_JumpTo_1.%20%20Introduction">
                   <span>1.  Introduction</span>
                </a>
            </li>
            <li>
                <a title="2.  Experimental Section" class="article__jump__link internalNav" href="#_i3" data-id="article_stickyheader_JumpTo_2.%20%20Experimental%20Section">
                   <span>2.  Experimental Section</span>
                </a>

        <script nonce="931888d23c4954b8-SJC">
(function(){
	
	/*Event Tracking for RIS menu dropdown - Article pages*/

	$('.cit-download-dropdown_button').on('click',function(e)
	{	
		e.preventDefault();
	    gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':'Main'});

	});


	$('.cit-download-dropdown_content ul li').on('click',function(e)
	{	
	//	e.preventDefault();
	    var idx = $(this).index();
	    
	    var label = '';
	    if (idx === 0 ) {
	    	label = 'Citation';
	    } else if (idx === 1 ) {
	    	label = 'Abstract';
	    } else if (idx === 2 ) {
	    	label = 'Reference';
	    } else if (idx === 3 ) {
	    	label = 'More Options';
	    }
	   
	   gtag('event', 'Export', { 'event_category': 'Citation', 'event_label':label});
	});

	$('.header_contnav-next a').on('click', function(e){
        e.preventDefault();
        gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Right Arrow'});
	}); 

 	$('.header_contnav-pre a').on('click', function(e){
	    e.preventDefault();
	     gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Left Arrow'});
	}); 

	$('.share_button').on('click', function(e){
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Top', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Share'});
	});

	$("#pane-m1con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Figures'});
	});

	$("#pane-m2con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();

	});

	$("#pane-m3con").on('click', function(e) {
		e.preventDefault();
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Supporting Info'});
	});

	$('.tab__close').on('click', function(){
		gtag('event', 'Sidebar', { 'event_category': 'Article', 'event_label':'Close'});
	}); 



})();

<script nonce="931888d23c4954b8-SJC">
document.addEventListener('DOMContentLoaded', function () {
    const modalContainer = document.querySelector('.my-main-container');

    // Function to show the modal
    function showModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'flex';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'block';
    }

    // Function to hide the modal when the close button is clicked
    function hideModal() {
        modalContainer.style.display = 'none';
        document.querySelector('.overlay').style.display = 'none';
    }

    // Hide the modal initially
    hideModal();

    // Function to handle clicks outside the modal
    function handleClickOutside(event) {
        // const modalContainer = document.querySelector(".my-main-container");
        // Check if the click target is not a child of the modal container
        if (!modalContainer.contains(event.target)) {
            hideModal();
        }
    }

    // Add a click event listener to the close button
    document
        .querySelector('.close-button')
        .addEventListener('click', hideModal);

    // Add a click event listener to the document to handle clicks outside the modal
    document.addEventListener('click', handleClickOutside);

    const screenHeight = window.innerHeight;
    function updateModalPosition() {
        const scrollY = window.scrollY;
        // console.log("scrollY= " + scrollY);
        // modalContainer.style.top = `${50 + scrollY}px`;
        modalContainer.style.top = `${scrollY + screenHeight / 2}px`;
        // console.log("new top= " + modalContainer.style.top);
    }

    // Update the modal position when the user scrolls
    window.addEventListener('scroll', updateModalPosition);

    // Initialize the modal position
    updateModalPosition();

    //---Cookie---
    // Function to set the cookie with a given name, value, and expiration time in days
    function setCookie(name, value, days) {
        var expires = '';
        if (days) {
            var date = new Date();
            date.setTime(date.getTime() + days * 24 * 60 * 60 * 1000);
            expires = '; expires=' + date.toUTCString();
            console.log(expires + 'this is expires dateUTC');
        }
        document.cookie = name + '=' + (value || '') + expires + '; path=/';
        // console.log("document.cookie is: " + document.cookie);
    }

    if (!document.cookie.includes('modalAd')) {
        // Show Modal
        showModal();

        // Set the cookie with the name "modalAd", value "true", and expiration of 3 day
        setCookie('modalAd', 'true', 3);
    } else {
        hideModal();
    }
});

</script>
<link rel="stylesheet" type="text/css" href="/pb-assets/smartgroup_ads/Modal-LeadGen-1691422642793.css">
            
        </div>

<script nonce="931888d23c4954b8-SJC">
    document.addEventListener("DOMContentLoaded", function(){
    // Function to get a Human readable formatted date
    function getFormattedDateForMaintenance() {
        let today = new Date();
        let dd = String(today.getDate()).padStart(2, '0');
        //January is 0
        let mm = String(today.getMonth() + 1).padStart(2, '0'); 
        let yyyy = today.getFullYear();
        
        let daysOfWeek = ['Sunday', 'Monday', 'Tuesday', 'Wednesday', 'Thursday', 'Friday', 'Saturday'];
        let monthsOfYear = ['January', 'February', 'March', 'April', 'May', 'June', 'July', 'August', 'September', 'October', 'November', 'December'];

        let dayOfWeek = daysOfWeek[today.getDay()];
        let monthOfYear = monthsOfYear[today.getMonth()];

        return dayOfWeek + ', ' + monthOfYear + ' ' + dd + ', ' + yyyy;
    }
    
    // Insert the date into the .system-message-date element
    document.querySelector('.system-message-date').innerText = getFormattedDateForMaintenance();
});
</script>

      function init() {
        setTimeout(function () {
          var cookieExist = getCookie("gdpr");
          if (cookieExist) {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie exist");
            return false;
          } else {
            //console.log("gdpr cookie does not exist")
            gdprMessage();
          }
          //Events

          //Handle submit
          $("#gdpr-message-btn").on("click", function (event) {
            handleClick();

        //console.log("Show GDPR Message");
        var tMessage, emailInput, messageButton, ewrap;

        $("#gdpr-message-modal").show();
      }

      function handleClick() {
        //console.log($("#gdpr-input").val());
        emailValid = validateEmail($("#gdpr-input").val());
        if (emailValid) {
          //Submit the form
          //Hide form
          if ($(".gdpr_err")) {
            $(".gdpr_err").hide();
          }

          $("#gdpr-message-content");
          //If successful then...
          setCookie("gdpr", "true");
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid + " submiting form");

          gdprErrorMessage();
          //console.log("emailValid " + emailValid);
        }

      function gdprErrorMessage() {
        if (errorStatus) {
          return false;
        } else {
          errorStatus = true;
          $("#gdpr-message-content").append(errormessage);
        }

      function validateEmail(email) {
        var re =
          /^(([^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+(\.[^<>()\[\]\\.,;:\s@"]+)*)|(".+"))@((\[[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\.[0-9]{1,3}\])|(([a-zA-Z\-0-9]+\.)+[a-zA-Z]{2,}))$/;
        return re.test(String(email).toLowerCase());
      }

      function setCookie(name, value) {
        var expires = "";
        expires = "; expires=Fri, 31 Dec 9999 23:59:59 GMT";
        document.cookie = name + "=" + (value || "") + expires + "; path=/";
      }

      function getCookie(name) {
        var match = document.cookie.match(new RegExp(name + "=([^;]+)"));
        if (match) return match[1];
      }

      function hideForm() {
        //And set cookie
        $("#gdpr-message-modal").hide();
        setCookie("gdpr", "false");
        gtag("event", "GDPR Modal", {
          event_category: "Pubs GDPR",
          event_label: "GDPR Dismissal",
        });
      }

      return {
        init: init,
      };
    })();

  </script>

        <script nonce="931888d23c4954b8-SJC">
var currentJournal = "ACS Applied Materials &amp;amp; Interfaces";
$(".citationLinks > a").click(function(e){
    if($(this).text() === "ACS Full Text ") {
        gtag('event', 'ACS Full Text', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Crossref") {
        gtag('event', 'Crossref', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "CAS") {
        gtag('event', 'CAS', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "PubMed") {
        gtag('event', 'PubMed', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).text() === "Google Scholar") {
        gtag('event', 'Google Scholar', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
    else if($(this).hasClass("issue-item_open-url") === true) {
        gtag('event', 'OpenURL', { 'event_category': 'Reference _Click', 'event_label':currentJournal});
    }
});
</script>
